He also asserted that he could be fair and require the government to prove its case, that he did not believe everything he read in the paper, that he did not 'get into the details' of the Enron coverage, that he did not watch television, and that Enron was 'old news.'&nbsp;"  554 F.&nbsp;3d, at 563-564.</p>
</blockquote><p>
Despite his criticism of greed, Juror 11 remarked that Skilling "earned [his] salar[y]," App. 857a, and said he would have "no problem" telling his co-worker, who had lost 401(k) funds due to Enron's collapse, that the jury voted to acquit, if that scenario came to pass, <em>id.,</em> at 854a.  The District Court, noting that it had "looked [Juror 11] in the eye and ... heard all his [answers]," found his assertions of impartiality credible.  <em>Id.</em>, at 858a; cf. <em>supra</em>, at 29, n. 30.  We agree with the Court of Appeals that "[t]he express finding that Juror 11 was fair is not reversible error."  554 F.&nbsp;3d, at 564.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 33" name="FRopinion1.33" href="#FNopinion1.33">33</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Skilling also objected at trial to the seating of six specific jurors whom, he said, he would have excluded had he not already exhausted his peremptory challenges.  See <em>supra</em>, at 8-9.  Juror 20, he observes, "said she was 'angry' about Enron's collapse and that she, too, had been 'forced to forfeit [her] own 401(k) funds to survive layoffs.'&nbsp;"  Reply Brief 13.  But Juror 20 made clear during <em>voir dire </em>that she did not "personally blame" Skilling for the loss of her retirement account.  App. 875a.  Having not "pa[id] much attention" to Enron-related news, she "quite honestly" did not "have enough information to know" whether Skilling was probably guilty, <em>id.,</em> at 873a, and she "th[ought] [she] could be" fair and impartial, <em>id.,</em> at 875a.  In light of these answers, the District Court did not commit manifest error in finding Juror 20 fit for jury service.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The same is true of Juror 63, who, Skilling points out, wrote on her questionnaire "that [Skilling] 'probably knew [he] w[as] breaking the law.'&nbsp;"  Reply Brief 13.  During <em>voir dire</em>, however, Juror 63 insisted that she did not "really have an opinion [about Skilling's guilt] either way," App. 936a; she did not "know what [she] was thinking" when she completed the questionnaire, but she "absolutely" presumed Skilling innocent and confirmed her understanding that the Government would "have to prove" his guilt, <em>id.,</em> at 937a.  In response to follow-up questions from Skilling's counsel, she again stated she would not presume that Skilling violated any laws and could "[a]bsolutely" give her word that she could be fair.  <em>Id.,</em> at 937a-938a.  "Jurors," we have recognized, "cannot be expected invariably to express themselves carefully or even consistently."  <em>Yount</em>, 467 U.&nbsp;S., at 1039.  See also <em>id.,</em> at 1040 ("It is here that the federal [appellate] court's deference must operate, for while the cold record arouses some concern, only the trial judge could tell which of these answers was said with the greatest comprehension and certainty.").  From where we sit, we cannot conclude that Juror 63 was biased.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The four remaining jurors Skilling said he would have excluded with extra peremptory strikes exhibited no sign of prejudice we can discern.  See App. 891a-892a (Juror 38) (remembered no media coverage about Enron and said nothing in her experience would prevent her from being fair and impartial); Supp. App. 131sa-133sa, 136sa (Juror 67) (had no connection to Enron and no anger about its collapse); App. 969a (Juror 78) (did not "know much about" Enron); Supp. App. 165sa, App. 971a (Juror 84) (had not heard or read anything about Enron and said she did not "know enough to answer" the question whether she was angry about the company's demise).  Skilling's counsel declined to ask follow-up questions of any of these jurors and, indeed, told Juror 84 he had nothing to ask because she "gave all the right answers."  <em>Id.,</em> at 972a.  Whatever Skilling's reasons for wanting to strike these four individuals from his jury, he cannot credibly assert they displayed a disqualifying bias.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 34" name="FRopinion1.34" href="#FNopinion1.34">34</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, Skilling failed to establish that a presumption of prejudice arose or that actual bias infected the jury that tried him.  Jurors, the trial court correctly comprehended, need not enter the box with empty heads in order to determine the facts impartially.  "It is sufficient if the juror[s] can lay aside [their] impression[s] or opinion[s] and render a verdict based on the evidence presented in court."  <em>Irvin</em>, 366 U.&nbsp;S., at 723.  Taking account of the full record, rather than incomplete exchanges selectively culled from it, we find no cause to upset the lower courts' judgment that Skilling's jury met that measure.  We therefore affirm the Fifth Circuit's ruling that Skilling received a fair trial.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 35" name="FRopinion1.35" href="#FNopinion1.35">35</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We next consider whether Skilling's conspiracy conviction was premised on an improper theory of honest-services wire fraud.  The honest-services statute, 1346, Skilling maintains, is unconstitutionally vague.  Alternatively, he contends that his conduct does not fall within the statute's compass.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To place Skilling's constitutional challenge in context, we first review the origin and subsequent application of the honest-services doctrine.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Enacted in 1872, the original mail-fraud provision, the predecessor of the modern-day mail- and wire-fraud laws, proscribed, without further elaboration, use of the mails to advance "any scheme or artifice to defraud."  See <em>McNally</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S. 350, 356 (1987).  In 1909, Congress amended the statute to prohibit, as it does today, "any scheme or artifice to defraud, <em>or for obtaining money or property by means of false or fraudulent pretenses, representations, or promises</em>."  1341 (emphasis added); see <em>id.</em>, at 357-358.  Emphasizing Congress' disjunctive phrasing, the Courts of Appeals, one after the other, interpreted the term "scheme or artifice to defraud" to include deprivations not only of money or property, but also of intangible rights.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In an opinion credited with first presenting the intangible-rights theory, <em>Shushan </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 117 F.&nbsp;2d 110 (1941),<em> </em>the Fifth Circuit reviewed the mail-fraud prosecution of a public official who allegedly accepted bribes from entrepreneurs in exchange for urging city action beneficial to the bribe payers.  "It is not true that because the [city] was to make and did make a saving by the operations there could not have been an intent to defraud," the Court of Appeals maintained.  <em>Id.,</em> at 119.  "A scheme to get a public contract on more favorable terms than would likely be got otherwise by bribing a public official," the court observed, "would not only be a plan to commit the crime of bribery, but would also be a scheme to defraud the public."  <em>Id.,</em> at 115.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Fifth Circuit's opinion in <em>Shushan</em> stimulated the development of an "honest-services" doctrine.  Unlike fraud in which the victim's loss of money or property supplied the defendant's gain, with one the mirror image of the other, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States </em>v. <em>Starr</em>, 816 F.&nbsp;2d 94, 101 (CA2 1987), the honest-services theory targeted corruption that lacked similar symmetry.  While the offender profited, the betrayed party suffered no deprivation of money or property; instead, a third party, who had not been deceived, provided the enrichment.  For example, if a city mayor (the offender) accepted a bribe from a third party in exchange for awarding that party a city contract, yet the contract terms were the same as any that could have been negotiated at arm's length, the city (the betrayed party) would suffer no tangible loss.  Cf. <em>McNally</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S., at 360.  Even if the scheme occasioned a money or property <em>gain </em>for the betrayed party, courts reasoned, actionable harm lay in the denial of that party's right to the offender's "honest services."  See, <em>e.g., United States</em> v. <em>Dixon</em>, 536 F.&nbsp;2d 1388, 1400 (CA2 1976).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Most often these cases ... involved bribery of public officials," <em>United States</em> v. <em>Bohonus</em>, 628 F.&nbsp;2d 1167, 1171 (CA9 1980), but courts also recognized private-sector honest-services fraud.  In perhaps the earliest application of the theory to private actors, a District Court, reviewing a bribery scheme, explained:</p>
<blockquote><p>"When one tampers with [the employer-employee] relationship for the purpose of causing the employee to breach his duty [to his employer,] he in effect is defrauding the employer of a lawful right.  The actual deception that is practised is in the continued representation of the employee to the employer that he is honest and loyal to the employer's interests."  <em>United States</em> v. <em>Procter &amp; Gamble Co.</em>, 47 F.&nbsp;Supp. 676, 678 (Mass. 1942).</p>
</blockquote><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Over time, "[a]n increasing number of courts" recognized that "a recreant employee"--public or private--"c[ould] be prosecuted under [the mail-fraud statute] if he breache[d] his allegiance to his employer by accepting bribes or kickbacks in the course of his employment," <em>United States</em> v. <em>McNeive</em>, 536 F.&nbsp;2d 1245, 1249 (CA8 1976); by 1982, all Courts of Appeals had embraced the honest-services theory of fraud, Hurson, Limiting the Federal Mail Fraud Statute--A Legislative Approach, 20 Am. Crim. L.&nbsp;Rev. 423, 456 (1983).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 36" name="FRopinion1.36" href="#FNopinion1.36">36</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In 1987, this Court, in <em>McNally</em> v. <em>United States</em>, stopped the development of the intangible-rights doctrine in its tracks.  <em>McNally</em> involved a state officer who, in selecting Kentucky's insurance agent, arranged to procure a share of the agent's commissions via kickbacks paid to companies the official partially controlled.  483 U.&nbsp;S., at 360.  The prosecutor did not charge that, "in the absence of the alleged scheme[,] the Commonwealth would have paid a lower premium or secured better insurance." <em>Ibid.</em>  Instead, the prosecutor maintained that the kickback scheme "defraud[ed] the citizens and government of Kentucky of their right to have the Commonwealth's affairs conducted honestly."  <em>Id.,</em> at 353.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We held that the scheme did not qualify as mail fraud.  "Rather than constru[ing] the statute in a manner that leaves its outer boundaries ambiguous and involves the Federal Government in setting standards of disclosure and good government for local and state officials," we read the statute "as limited in scope to the protection of property rights."  <em>Id.</em>, at 360.  "If Congress desires to go further," we stated, "it must speak more clearly."  <em>Ibid</em>.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">3</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Congress responded swiftly.  The following year, it enacted a new statute "specifically to cover one of the 'intangible rights' that lower courts had protected ... prior to <em>McNally:</em> 'the intangible right of honest services.'&nbsp;"  <em>Cleveland</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 531 U.&nbsp;S. 12, 19-20 (2000).  In full, the honest-services statute stated:</p>
<blockquote><p>"For the purposes of th[e] chapter [of the United States Code that prohibits, <em>inter alia</em>, mail fraud, 1341, and wire fraud, 1343], the term 'scheme or artifice to defraud' includes a scheme or artifice to deprive another of the intangible right of honest services."  1346.</p>
</blockquote><h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Congress, Skilling charges, reacted quickly but not clearly: He asserts that 1346 is unconstitutionally vague.  To satisfy due process, "a penal statute [must] define the criminal offense [1] with sufficient definiteness that ordinary people can understand what conduct is prohibited and [2] in a manner that does not encourage arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement."  <em>Kolender</em> v. <em>Lawson</em>, 461 U.&nbsp;S. 352, 357 (1983).  The void-for-vagueness doctrine embraces these requirements.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;According to Skilling, 1346 meets neither of the two due process essentials.  First, the phrase "the intangible right of honest services," he contends, does not adequately define what behavior it bars.  Brief for Petitioner 38-39.  Second, he alleges, 1346's "standardless sweep allows policemen, prosecutors, and juries to pursue their personal predilections," thereby "facilitat[ing] opportunistic and arbitrary prosecutions."  <em>Id.,</em> at 44 (quoting <em>Kolender</em>, 461 U.&nbsp;S., at 358).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In urging invalidation of 1346, Skilling swims against our case law's current, which requires us, if we can, to construe, not condemn, Congress' enactments.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Civil Service Comm'n</em> v. <em>Letter Carriers</em>, 413 U.&nbsp;S. 548, 571 (1973).  See also <em>United States</em> v. <em>National Dairy Products Corp.</em>, 372 U.&nbsp;S. 29, 32 (1963) (stressing, in response to a vagueness challenge, "[t]he strong presumptive validity that attaches to an Act of Congress"). Alert to 1346's potential breadth, the Courts of Appeals have divided on how best to interpret the statute.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 37" name="FRopinion1.37" href="#FNopinion1.37">37</a></sup>  Uniformly, however, they have declined to throw out the statute as irremediably vague.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 38" name="FRopinion1.38" href="#FNopinion1.38">38</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We agree that 1346 should be construed rather than invalidated.  First, we look to the doctrine developed in pre-<em>McNally </em>cases in an endeavor to ascertain the meaning of the phrase "the intangible right of honest services."  Second, to preserve what Congress certainly intended the statute to cover, we pare that body of precedent down to its core: In the main, the pre-<em>McNally </em>cases involved fraudulent schemes to deprive another of honest services through bribes or kickbacks supplied by a third party who had not been deceived.  Confined to these paramount applications, 1346 presents no vagueness problem.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There is no doubt that Congress intended 1346 to refer to and incorporate the honest-services doctrine recognized in Court of Appeals' decisions before <em>McNally</em> derailed the intangible-rights theory of fraud.  See Brief for Petitioner 39; Brief for United States 37-38; <em>post</em>, at 2, 8 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia, J.</small></span>, concurring in part and concurring in judgment).  Congress enacted 1346 on the heels of <em>McNally</em> and drafted the statute using that decision's<em> </em>terminology.  See 483 U.&nbsp;S., at 355 ("intangible righ[t]"); <em>id.,</em> at 362 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Stevens, J.</small></span>, dissenting) ("right to ... honest services").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 39" name="FRopinion1.39" href="#FNopinion1.39">39</a></sup>  As the Second Circuit observed in its leading analysis of 1346:</p>
<blockquote><p>"The definite article 'the' suggests that 'intangible right of honest services' had a specific meaning to Congress when it enacted the statute--Congress was recriminalizing mail- and wire-fraud schemes to deprive others of <em>that</em> 'intangible right of honest services,' which had been protected before <em>McNally</em>, not <em>all</em> intangible rights of honest services whatever they might be thought to be."  <em>United States</em> v. <em>Rybicki</em>, 354 F.&nbsp;3d 124, 137-138 (2003) (en banc).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 40" name="FRopinion1.40" href="#FNopinion1.40">40</a></sup></p>
</blockquote><h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Satisfied that Congress, by enacting 1346, "meant to reinstate the body of pre-<em>McNally</em> honest-services law," <em>post</em>, at 8 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia, J.</small></span>), we have surveyed that case law.  See <em>infra</em>, at 43-44, 46.  In parsing the Courts of Appeals decisions, we acknowledge that Skilling's vagueness challenge has force, for honest-services decisions preceding <em>McNally</em> were not models of clarity or consistency.  See Brief for Petitioner 39-42 (describing divisions of opinions).  See also <em>post</em>, at 3-7 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia, J.</small></span>).  While the honest-services cases preceding <em>McNally </em>dominantly and consistently applied the fraud statute to bribery and kickback schemes--schemes that were the basis of most honest-services prosecutions--there was considerable disarray over the statute's application to conduct outside that core category.  In light of this disarray, Skilling urges us, as he urged the Fifth Circuit, to invalidate the statute <em>in toto</em>.  Brief for Petitioner 48 (Section 1346 "is intolerably and unconstitutionally vague."); Brief of Defendant-Appellant Jeffrey K. Skilling in No. 06-20885 (CA5), p. 65, n. 21 ("[S]ection 1346 should be invalidated as unlawfully vague on its face.").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It has long been our practice, however, before striking a federal statute as impermissibly vague, to consider whether the prescription is amenable to a limiting construction.  See, e.g., <em>Hooper</em> v. <em>California</em>, 155 U.&nbsp;S. 648, 657 (1895) ("The elementary rule is that <em>every reasonable construction</em> must be resorted to, in order to save a statute from unconstitutionality." (emphasis added)).  See also <em>Boos</em> v. <em>Barry</em>, 485 U.&nbsp;S. 312, 330-331 (1988); <em>Schneider</em> v. <em>Smith</em>, 390 U.&nbsp;S. 17, 26 (1968).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 41" name="FRopinion1.41" href="#FNopinion1.41">41</a></sup>  We have accordingly instructed "the federal courts ... to avoid constitutional difficulties by [adopting a limiting interpretation] if such a construction is fairly possible."  <em>Boos</em>, 485 U.&nbsp;S., at 331; see <em>United States</em> v. <em>Harriss</em>, 347 U.&nbsp;S. 612, 618 (1954) ("[I]f the general class of offenses to which the statute is directed is plainly within its terms, the statute will not be struck down as vague ...&nbsp;.  And if this general class of offenses can be made constitutionally definite by a reasonable construction of the statute, this Court is under a duty to give the statute that construction.").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Arguing against any limiting construction, Skilling contends that it is impossible to identify a salvageable honest-services core; "the pre-<em>McNally </em>caselaw," he asserts, "is a hodgepodge of oft-conflicting holdings" that are "hopelessly unclear."  Brief for Petitioner 39 (some capitalization and italics omitted).  We have rejected an argument of the same tenor before.  In <em>Civil Service Comm'n</em> v. <em>Letter Carriers</em>, federal employees challenged a provision of the Hatch Act that incorporated earlier decisions of the United States Civil Service Commission enforcing a similar law.  "[T]he several thousand adjudications of the Civil Service Commission," the employees maintained, were "an impenetrable jungle"--"undiscoverable, inconsistent, [and] incapable of yielding any meaningful rules to govern present or future conduct."  413 U.&nbsp;S., at 571.  Mindful that "our task [wa]s not to destroy the Act if we c[ould], but to construe it," we held that "the rules that had evolved over the years from repeated adjudications were subject to sufficiently clear and summary statement."  <em>Id.,</em> at 571-572.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A similar observation may be made here.  Although some applications of the pre-<em>McNally </em>honest-services doctrine occasioned disagreement among the Courts of Appeals, these cases do not cloud the doctrine's solid core: The "vast majority" of the honest-services cases involved offenders who, in violation of a fiduciary duty, participated in bribery or kickback schemes.  <em>United States </em>v. <em>Runnels</em>, 833 F.&nbsp;2d 1183, 1187 (CA6 1987); see Brief for United States 42, and n. 4 (citing dozens of examples).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 42" name="FRopinion1.42" href="#FNopinion1.42">42</a></sup>  Indeed, the <em>McNally</em> case itself, which spurred Congress to enact 1346, presented a paradigmatic kickback fact pattern.  483 U.&nbsp;S., at 352-353, 360.  Congress' reversal of <em>McNally </em>and reinstatement of the honest-services doctrine, we conclude, can and should be salvaged by confining its scope to the core pre-<em>McNally</em> applications.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As already noted, <em>supra, </em>at 35-36, the honest-services doctrine had its genesis in prosecutions involving bribery allegations.  See <em>Shushan</em>, 117 F.&nbsp;2d, at 115 (public sector); <em>Procter &amp; Gamble Co.</em>, 47 F.&nbsp;Supp., at 678 (private sector).  See also <em>United States</em> v. <em>Orsburn</em>, 525 F.&nbsp;3d 543, 546 (CA7 2008).  Both before <em>McNally </em>and after 1346's enactment, Courts of Appeals described schemes involving bribes or kickbacks as "core ... honest services fraud precedents," <em>United States</em> v. <em>Czubinski</em>, 106 F.&nbsp;3d 1069, 1077 (CA1 1997); "paradigm case[s]," <em>United States</em> v. <em>deVegter</em>, 198 F.&nbsp;3d 1324, 1327-1328 (CA11 1999); "[t]he most obvious form of honest services fraud," <em>United States</em> v. <em>Carbo</em>, 572 F.&nbsp;3d 112, 115 (CA3 2009); "core misconduct covered by the statute," <em>United States</em> v. <em>Urciuoli</em>, 513 F.&nbsp;3d 290, 294 (CA1 2008); "most [of the] honest services cases," <em>United States </em>v. <em>Sorich</em>, 523 F.&nbsp;3d 702, 707 (CA7 2008); "typical," <em>United States</em> v. <em>Brown</em>, 540 F.&nbsp;2d 364, 374 (CA8 1976); "clear-cut," <em>United States</em> v. <em>Mandel</em>, 591 F.&nbsp;2d 1347, 1363 (CA4 1979); and "uniformly ... cover[ed]," <em>United States</em> v. <em>Paradies</em>, 98 F.&nbsp;3d 1266, 1283, n. 30 (CA11 1996).  See also Tr. of Oral Arg. 43 (counsel for the Government) ("[T]he bulk of pre-<em>McNally</em> honest services cases" entailed bribes or kickbacks); Brief for Petitioner 49 ("Bribes and kickbacks were <em>the</em> paradigm [pre-<em>McNally</em>] cases," constituting "[t]he overwhelming majority of prosecutions for honest services fraud.").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In view of this history, there is no doubt that Congress intended 1346 to reach <em>at least</em> bribes and kickbacks.  Reading the statute to proscribe a wider range of offensive conduct, we acknowledge, would raise the due process concerns underlying the vagueness doctrine.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 43" name="FRopinion1.43" href="#FNopinion1.43">43</a></sup>  To preserve the statute without transgressing constitutional limitations, we now hold that 1346 criminalizes <em>only</em> the bribe-and-kickback core of the pre-<em>McNally </em>case law.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 44" name="FRopinion1.44" href="#FNopinion1.44">44</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">3</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Government urges us to go further by locating within 1346's compass another category of proscribed conduct: "undisclosed self-dealing by a public official or private employee--<em>i.e.</em>, the taking of official action by the employee that furthers his own undisclosed financial interests while purporting to act in the interests of those to whom he owes a fiduciary duty."  <em>Id.</em>, at 43-44.  "[T]he theory of liability in <em>McNally</em> itself was nondisclosure of a conflicting financial interest," the Government observes, and "Congress clearly intended to revive th[at] nondisclosure theory."  <em>Id.,</em> at 44.  Moreover, "[a]lthough not as numerous as the bribery and kickback cases," the Government asserts, "the pre-<em>McNally</em> cases involving undisclosed self-dealing were abundant."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Neither of these contentions withstands close inspection.  <em>McNally</em>, as we have already observed, <em>supra</em>, at 37, 43, involved a classic kickback scheme: A public official, in exchange for routing Kentucky's insurance business through a middleman company, arranged for that company to share its commissions with entities in which the official held an interest.  483 U.&nbsp;S., at 352-353, 360.  This was no mere failure to disclose a conflict of interest; rather, the official conspired with a third party so that both would profit from wealth generated by public contracts.  See <em>id.</em>, at 352-353.  Reading 1346 to proscribe bribes and kickbacks--and nothing more--satisfies Congress' undoubted aim to reverse <em>McNally</em> on its facts.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor are we persuaded that the pre-<em>McNally</em> conflict-of-interest cases constitute core applications of the honest-services doctrine.  Although the Courts of Appeals upheld honest-services convictions for "some schemes of non-disclosure and concealment of material information," <em>Mandel</em>, 591 F.&nbsp;2d, at 1361, they reached no consensus on which schemes qualified.  In light of the relative infrequency of conflict-of-interest prosecutions in comparison to bribery and kickback charges, and the intercircuit inconsistencies they produced, we conclude that a reasonable limiting construction of 1346 must exclude this amorphous category of cases.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Further dispelling doubt on this point is the familiar principle that "ambiguity concerning the ambit of criminal statutes should be resolved in favor of lenity."  <em>Cleveland</em>, 531 U.&nbsp;S., at 25 (quoting <em>Rewis</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 401 U.&nbsp;S. 808, 812 (1971)).  "This interpretive guide is especially appropriate in construing [1346] because ... mail [and wire] fraud [are] predicate offense[s] under [the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act], 18 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1961(1) (1994 ed., Supp. IV), and the money laundering statute, 1956(c)(7)(A)."  <em>Cleveland</em>, 531 U.&nbsp;S., at 25<em>.</em>  Holding that honest-services fraud does not encompass conduct more wide-ranging than the paradigmatic cases of bribes and kickbacks, we resist the Government's less constrained construction absent Congress' clear instruction otherwise.  <em>E.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Universal C. I. T. Credit Corp.</em>, 344 U.&nbsp;S. 218, 221-222 (1952).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, our construction of 1346 "establish[es] a uniform national standard, define[s] honest services with clarity, reach[es] only seriously culpable conduct, and accomplish[es] Congress's goal of 'overruling' <em>McNally</em>."  Brief for Albert W. Alschuler as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> in <em>Weyhrauch</em> v. <em>United States</em>, O. T. 2009, No. 08-1196, pp. 28-29.  "If Congress desires to go further," we reiterate, "it must speak more clearly than it has."  <em>McNally</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S., at 360.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 45" name="FRopinion1.45" href="#FNopinion1.45">45</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">4</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Interpreted to encompass only bribery and kickback schemes, 1346 is not unconstitutionally vague. Recall that the void-for-vagueness doctrine addresses concerns about (1) fair notice and (2) arbitrary and discriminatory prosecutions.  See <em>Kolender</em>, 461 U.&nbsp;S., at 357.  A prohibition on fraudulently depriving another of one's honest services by accepting bribes or kickbacks does not present a problem on either score.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As to fair notice, "whatever the school of thought concerning the scope and meaning of&nbsp;" 1346, it has always been "as plain as a pikestaff that" bribes and kickbacks constitute honest-services fraud, <em>Williams</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 341 U.&nbsp;S. 97, 101 (1951), and the statute's <em>mens rea</em> requirement further blunts any notice concern, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Screws</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 325 U.&nbsp;S. 91, 101-104 (1945) (plurality opinion).  See also <em>Broadrick</em> v. <em>Oklahoma</em>, 413 U.&nbsp;S. 601, 608 (1973) ("[E]ven if the outermost boundaries of [a statute are] imprecise, any such uncertainty has little relevance ... where appellants' conduct falls squarely within the 'hard core' of the statute's proscriptions.").  Today's decision clarifies that no other misconduct falls within 1346's province.  See <em>United States</em> v. <em>Lanier</em>, 520 U.&nbsp;S. 259, 266 (1997) ("[C]larity at the requisite level may be supplied by judicial gloss on an otherwise uncertain statute.").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As to arbitrary prosecutions, we perceive no significant risk that the honest-services statute, as we interpret it today, will be stretched out of shape.  Its prohibition on bribes and kickbacks draws content not only from the pre-<em>McNally</em> case law, but also from federal statutes proscribing--and defining--similar crimes.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> 18 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 201(b), 666(a)(2); 41 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 52(2) ("The term 'kickback' means any money, fee, commission, credit, gift, gratuity, thing of value, or compensation of any kind which is provided, directly or indirectly, to [enumerated persons] for the purpose of improperly obtaining or rewarding favorable treatment in connection with [enumerated circumstances].").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 46" name="FRopinion1.46" href="#FNopinion1.46">46</a></sup>  See also, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Ganim</em>, 510 F.&nbsp;3d 134, 147-149 (CA2 2007) (Sotomayor, J.) (reviewing honest-services conviction involving bribery in light of elements of bribery under other federal statutes); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Whitfield</em>, 590 F.&nbsp;3d 325, 352-353 (CA5 2009); <em>United States </em>v. <em>Kemp</em>, 500 F.&nbsp;3d 257, 281-286 (CA3 2007).  A criminal defendant who participated in a bribery or kickback scheme, in short, cannot tenably complain about prosecution under 1346 on vagueness grounds. </p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It remains to determine whether Skilling's conduct violated 1346.  Skilling's honest-services prosecution, the Government concedes, was not "prototypical."  Brief for United States 49.  The Government charged Skilling with conspiring to defraud Enron's shareholders by misrepresenting the company's fiscal health, thereby artificially inflating its stock price.  It was the Government's theory at trial that Skilling "profited from the fraudulent scheme ... through the receipt of salary and bonuses, ... and through the sale of approximately $200 million in Enron stock, which netted him $89 million."  <em>Id.,</em> at 51.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Government did not, at any time, allege that Skilling solicited or accepted side payments from a third party in exchange for making these misrepresentations.  See Record 41328 (May 11, 2006 Letter from the Government to the District Court) ("[T]he indictment does not allege, and the government's evidence did not show, that [Skilling] engaged in bribery.").  It is therefore clear that, as we read 1346, Skilling did not commit honest-services fraud.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Because the indictment alleged three objects of the conspiracy--honest-services wire fraud, money-or-property wire fraud, and securities fraud--Skilling's conviction is flawed.  See <em>Yates</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 354 U.&nbsp;S. 298 (1957) (constitutional error occurs when a jury is instructed on alternative theories of guilt and returns a general verdict that may rest on a legally invalid theory).  This determination, however, does not necessarily require reversal of the conspiracy conviction; we recently confirmed, in <em>Hedgpeth</em> v. <em>Pulido</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2008) <em>(per curiam)</em>, that errors of the <em>Yates</em> variety are subject to harmless-error analysis.<em>  </em>The parties vigorously dispute whether the error was harmless.  Compare Brief for United States 52 ("[A]ny juror who voted for conviction based on [the honest-services theory] also would have found [Skilling] guilty of conspiring to commit securities fraud.") with Reply Brief 30 (The Government "cannot show that the conspiracy conviction rested <em>only</em> on the securities-fraud theory, rather than the distinct, legally-flawed honest-services theory.").  We leave this dispute for resolution on remand.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 47" name="FRopinion1.47" href="#FNopinion1.47">47</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Whether potential reversal on the conspiracy count touches any of Skilling's other convictions is also an open question.  All of his convictions, Skilling contends, hinged on the conspiracy count and, like dominoes, must fall if it falls.  The District Court, deciding Skilling's motion for bail pending appeal, found this argument dubious, App. 1141a-1142a, but the Fifth Circuit had no occasion to rule on it.  That court may do so on remand.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">***</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For the foregoing reasons, we affirm the Fifth Circuit's ruling on Skilling's fair-trial argument, vacate its ruling on his conspiracy conviction, and remand the case for proceedings consistent with this opinion.</p>
<p align="RIGHT">It is so ordered.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<a name="concurrence1"></a>



<center><h2><p align="CENTER">JEFFREY K. SKILLING, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> UNITED STATES</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER">on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the fifth circuit</p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[June 24, 2010]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span>, with whom J<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>ustice</small></span> T<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>homas</small></span> joins, and with whom J<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>ustice</small></span> K<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>ennedy</small></span> joins except as to Part III, concurring in part and concurring in the judgment.</p>
<p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I agree with the Court that petitioner Jeffrey Skilling's challenge to the impartiality of his jury and to the District Court's conduct of the <em>voir dire</em> fails.  I therefore join Parts I and II of the Court's opinion.  I also agree that the decision upholding Skilling's conviction for so-called "honest-services fraud" must be reversed, but for a different reason.  In my view, the specification in 18 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1346 (2006 ed., Supp. II) that "scheme or artifice to defraud" in the mail-fraud and wire-fraud statutes, 1341 and 1343 (2006 ed.), includes "a scheme or artifice to deprive another of the intangible right of honest services," is vague, and therefore violates the Due Process Clause of the Fifth Amendment.  The Court strikes a pose of judicial humility in proclaiming that our task is "not to destroy the Act ... but to construe it," <em>ante</em>, at 43 (internal quotation marks omitted).  But in transforming the prohibition of "honest-services fraud" into a prohibition of "bribery and kick-backs" it is wielding a power we long ago abjured: the power to define new federal crimes.  See <em>United States</em> v. <em>Hudson,</em> 7 Cranch 32, 34 (1812).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">I</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A criminal statute must clearly define the conduct it proscribes, see <em>Grayned</em> v. <em>City of Rockford</em>, 408 U.&nbsp;S. 104, 108 (1972).  A statute that is unconstitutionally vague cannot be saved by a more precise indictment, see <em>Lanzetta</em> v. <em>New Jersey</em>, 306 U.&nbsp;S. 451, 453 (1939), nor by judicial construction that writes in specific criteria that its text does not contain, see <em>United States</em> v. <em>Reese</em>, 92 U.&nbsp;S. 214, 219-221 (1876).  Our cases have described vague statutes as failing "to provide a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice of what is prohibited, or [as being] so standardless that [they] authoriz[e] or encourag[e] seriously discriminatory enforcement."  <em>United States</em> v. <em>Williams</em>, 553 U.&nbsp;S. 285, 304 (2008).  Here, Skilling argues that 1346 fails to provide fair notice and encourages arbitrary enforcement because it provides no definition of the right of honest services whose deprivation it prohibits.  Brief for Petitioner 38-39, 42-44.  In my view Skilling is correct.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court maintains that "the intangible right of honest services&nbsp;" means the right not to have one's fiduciaries accept "bribes or kickbacks."  Its first step in reaching that&nbsp;conclusion is the assertion that the phrase refers to "the doctrine developed" in cases decided by lower federal courts prior to our decision in <em>McNally </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S. 350 (1987).  <em>Ante</em>, at 39.  I do not contest that.  I agree that Congress used the novel phrase to adopt the lower-court case law that had been disapproved by <em>McNally</em>--what the Court calls "the pre-<em>McNally </em>honest-services doctrine," <em>ante,</em> at 43.  The problem is that that doctrine provides no "ascertainable standard of guilt," <em>United States </em>v. <em>L. Cohen Grocery Co.</em>, 255 U.&nbsp;S. 81, 89 (1921), and certainly is not limited to "bribes or kickbacks."</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Investigation into the meaning of "the pre-<em>McNally </em>honest-services doctrine" might logically begin with <em>McNally</em> itself, which rejected it.  That case repudiated the many Court of Appeals holdings that had expanded the meaning of "fraud" in the mail-fraud and wire-fraud statutes beyond deceptive schemes to obtain property. <em> </em>483 U.&nbsp;S., at 360.  If the repudiated cases stood for a prohibition of "bribery and kickbacks," one would have expected those words to appear in the opinion's description of the cases.  In fact, they do not.  <em>Not at all</em>.  Nor did <em>McNally</em> even provide a consistent definition of the pre-existing theory of fraud it rejected.  It referred variously to a right of citizens "to have the [State]'s affairs conducted honestly," <em>id.,</em> at 353, to "honest and impartial government," <em>id.,</em> at 355, to "good government," <em>id.,</em> at 356, and "to have public officials perform their duties honestly," <em>id.,</em> at 358.  It described prior case law as holding that "a public official owes a fiduciary duty to the public, and misuse of his office for private gain is a fraud," <em>id.,</em> at 355.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But the pre-<em>McNally </em>Court of Appeals opinions were not limited to fraud by public officials.  Some courts had held that those fiduciaries subject to the "honest services" obligation included private individuals who merely participated in public decisions, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Gray</em>, 790 F.&nbsp;2d 1290, 1295-1296 (CA6 1986) (citing <em>United States</em> v. <em>Margiotta</em>, 688 F.&nbsp;2d 108, 122 (CA2 1982)), and even private employees who had no role in public decisions, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Lemire</em>, 720 F.&nbsp;2d 1327, 1335-1336 (CADC 1983); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Von Barta</em>, 635 F.&nbsp;2d 999, 1007 (CA2 1980).  Moreover, "to say that a man is a fiduciary only begins [the] analysis; it gives direction to further inquiry...&nbsp;.  What obligations does he owe as a fiduciary?"  <em>SEC </em>v. <em>Chenery Corp.</em>, 318 U.&nbsp;S. 80, 85-86 (1943).  None of the "honest services" cases, neither those pertaining to public officials nor those pertaining to private employees, defined the nature and content of the fiduciary duty central to the "fraud" offense.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There was not even universal agreement concerning the <em>source </em>of the fiduciary obligation--whether it must be positive state or federal law, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Rabbitt</em>, 583 F.&nbsp;2d 1014, 1026 (CA8 1978), or merely general principles, such as the "obligations of loyalty and fidelity" that inhere in the "employment relationship," <em>Lemire</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 1336.  The decision <em>McNally</em> reversed had grounded the duty in general (not jurisdiction-specific) trust law, see <em>Gray</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 1294, a <em>corpus juris</em> festooned with various duties.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> Restatement (Second) of Trusts 169-185 (1976).  Another pre-<em>McNally</em> case referred to the general law of agency, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Ballard</em>, 663 F.&nbsp;2d 534, 543, n. 22 (CA5 1981), modified on other grounds by 680 F.&nbsp;2d 352 (1982), which imposes duties quite different from those of a trustee.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 1" name="FRconcurrence1.1" href="#FNconcurrence1.1">1</a></sup>  See Restatement (Second) of Agency 377-398 (1957).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This indeterminacy does not disappear if one assumes that the pre-<em>McNally</em> cases developed a federal, common-law fiduciary duty; the duty remained hopelessly undefined.  Some courts described it in astoundingly broad language.  <em>Blachly</em> v. <em>United States, </em>380 F.&nbsp;2d 665 (CA5 1967), loftily declared that "[l]aw puts its imprimatur on the accepted moral standards and condemns conduct which fails to match the 'reflection of moral uprightness, of fundamental honesty, fair play and right dealing in the general and business life of members of society.'&nbsp;"  <em>Id.</em>, at 671 (quoting <em>Gregory</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 253 F.&nbsp;2d 104, 109 (CA5 1958)).  Other courts unhelpfully added that any scheme "contrary to public policy" was also condemned by the statute, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Bohonus</em>, 628 F.&nbsp;2d 1167, 1171 (CA9 1980).  See also <em>United States</em> v. <em>Mandel</em>, 591 F.&nbsp;2d 1347, 1361 (CA4 1979) (any scheme that is "contrary to public policy and conflicts with accepted standards of moral uprightness, fundamental honesty, fair play and right dealing").  Even opinions that did not indulge in such grandiloquence did not specify the duty at issue beyond loyalty or honesty, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Von Barta</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 1005-1006.  Moreover, the demands of the duty were said to be greater for public officials than for private employees, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Lemire</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 1337, n.&nbsp;13; <em>Ballard</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 541, n.&nbsp;17, but in what respects (or by how much) was never made clear.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The indefiniteness of the fiduciary duty is not all.  Many courts held that some <em>je-ne-sais-quoi </em>beyond a mere breach of fiduciary duty was needed to establish honest-services fraud.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Von Barta</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 1006 (collecting cases); <em>United States </em>v. <em>George</em>, 477 F.&nbsp;2d 508, 512 (CA7 1973).  There was, unsurprisingly, some dispute about that, at least in the context of acts by persons owing duties to the public.  See <em>United States</em> v. <em>Price,</em> 788 F.&nbsp;2d 234, 237 (CA4 1986).  And even among those courts that did require something additional where a public official was involved, there was disagreement as to what the addition should be.  For example, in <em>United States</em> v. <em>Bush</em>, 522 F.&nbsp;2d 641 (1975), the Seventh Circuit held that material misrepresentations and active concealment were enough, <em>id.,</em> at 647-648.  But in <em>Rabbitt</em>, 583 F.&nbsp;2d 1014, the Eighth Circuit held that actual harm to the State was needed, <em>id.,</em> at 1026.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Similar disagreements occurred with respect to private employees.  Courts disputed whether the defendant must use his fiduciary position for his own gain.  Compare <em>Lemire,</em> <em>supra</em>, at 1335 (yes), with <em>United States</em> v. <em>Bronston</em>, 658 F.&nbsp;2d 920, 926 (CA2 1981) (no).  One opinion upheld a mail-fraud conviction on the ground that the defendant's "failure to disclose his receipt of kickbacks and consulting fees from [his employer's] suppliers resulted in a breach of his fiduciary duties depriving his employer of his loyal and honest services."  <em>United States </em>v. <em>Bryza</em>, 522 F.&nbsp;2d 414, 422 (CA7 1975).  Another opinion, however, demanded more than an intentional failure to disclose: "There must be a failure to disclose something which in the knowledge or contemplation of the employee poses an independent business risk to the employer."  <em>Lemire</em>, 720 F. 2d, at 1337.  Other courts required that the victim suffer some loss, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Ballard</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 541-542--a proposition that, of course, other courts rejected, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v<em>. Newman</em>, 664 F.&nbsp;2d 12, 20 (CA2 1981); <em>United States </em>v.<em> O'Malley</em>, 535 F.&nbsp;2d 589, 592 (CA10 1976).  The Court's statement today that there was a deprivation of honest services even if "the scheme occasioned a money or property <em>gain</em> for the betrayed party," <em>ante</em>, at 36, is therefore true, except to the extent it is not.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In short, the first step in the Court's analysis--holding that "the intangible right of honest services" refers to "the honest-services doctrine recognized in Court of Appeals' decisions before <em>McNally</em>," <em>ante</em>, at 40--is a step out of the frying pan into the fire.  The pre-<em>McNally</em> cases provide no clear indication of what constitutes a denial of the right of honest services.  The possibilities range from any action that is contrary to public policy or otherwise immoral, to only the disloyalty of a public official or employee to his principal, to only the secret use of a perpetrator's position of trust in order to harm whomever he is beholden to.  The duty probably did not have to be rooted in state law, but maybe it did.  It might have been more demanding in the case of public officials, but perhaps not.  At the time 1346 was enacted there was no settled criterion for choosing among these options, for conclusively settling what was in and what was out.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 2" name="FRconcurrence1.2" href="#FNconcurrence1.2">2</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">II</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court is aware of all this.  It knows that adopting by reference "the pre-<em>McNally </em>honest-services doctrine," <em>ante</em>, at 43, is adopting by reference nothing more precise than the referring term itself ("the intangible right of honest services").  Hence the <em>deus ex machina:</em> "[W]e pare that body of precedent down to its core," <em>ante</em>, at 39.  Since the honest-services doctrine "had its genesis" in bribery prosecutions, and since several cases and counsel for Skilling referred to bribery and kickback schemes as "core" or "paradigm" or "typical" examples, or "[t]he most obvious form," of honest-services fraud, <em>ante</em>, at 43-44 (internal quotation marks omitted),<em> </em>and since two cases and counsel for the Government say that they formed the "vast majority," or "most" or at least "[t]he bulk" of honest-services cases, <em>ante</em>, at 43-44 (internal quotation marks omitted),<em> </em>THEREFORE it must be the case that they are <em>all</em> Congress meant by its reference to the honest-services doctrine.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even if that conclusion followed from its premises, it would not suffice to eliminate the vagueness of the statute.  It would solve (perhaps) the indeterminacy of what acts constitute a breach of the "honest services" obligation under the pre-<em>McNally </em>law.  But it would not solve the most fundamental indeterminacy: the character of the "fiduciary capacity" to which the bribery and kickback restriction applies.  Does it apply only to public officials?  Or in addition to private individuals who contract with the public?  Or to everyone, including the corporate officer here?  The pre-<em>McNally</em> case law does not provide an answer.  Thus, even with the bribery and kickback limitation the statute does not answer the question "What is the criterion of guilt?"</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But that is perhaps beside the point, because it is obvious that mere prohibition of bribery and kickbacks was not the intent of the statute.  To say that bribery and kickbacks represented "the core" of the doctrine, or that most cases applying the doctrine involved those offenses, is not to say that they <em>are </em>the doctrine.  All it proves is that the multifarious versions of the doctrine <em>overlap</em> with regard to those offenses.  But the doctrine itself is much more.  Among all the pre-<em>McNally</em> smrgsbord-offerings of varieties of honest-services fraud, <em>not one</em> is limited to bribery and kickbacks.  That is a dish the Court has cooked up all on its own.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thus, the Court's claim to "respec[t] the legislature," <em>ante</em>, at 45, n. 44 (emphasis deleted), is false.  It is entirely clear (as the Court and I agree) that Congress meant to reinstate the body of pre-<em>McNally </em>honest-services law; and entirely clear that that prohibited much more (though precisely what more is uncertain) than bribery and kickbacks.  Perhaps it is true that "Congress intended 1346 to reach <em>at least</em> bribes and kickbacks," <em>ante</em>, at 44.  That simply does not mean, as the Court now holds, that "1346 criminalizes <em>only</em>" bribery and kickbacks, <em>ante</em>, at 45.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Arriving at that conclusion requires not interpretation but invention.  The Court replaces a vague criminal standard that Congress adopted with a more narrow one (included within the vague one) that can pass constitutional muster.  I know of no precedent for such "paring down,"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 3" name="FRconcurrence1.3" href="#FNconcurrence1.3">3</a></sup> and it seems to me clearly beyond judicial power.  This is not, as the Court claims, <em>ante</em>, at 41, simply a matter of adopting a "limiting construction" in the face of potential unconstitutionality.  To do that, our cases have been careful to note, the narrowing construction must be "fairly possible," <em>Boos</em> v. <em>Barry</em>, 485 U.&nbsp;S. 312, 331 (1988), "reasonable," <em>Hooper</em> v. <em>California</em>, 155 U.&nbsp;S. 648, 657 (1895), or not "plainly contrary to the intent of Congress," <em>Edward J. DeBartolo Corp.</em> v. <em>Florida Gulf Coast Building &amp; Constr. Trades Council</em>, 485 U.&nbsp;S. 568, 575 (1988).  As we have seen (and the Court does not contest), <em>no court</em> before <em>McNally</em> concluded that the "deprivation of honest services" meant <em>only</em> the acceptance of bribes or kickbacks.  If it were a "fairly possible" or "reasonable" construction, not "contrary to the intent of Congress," one would think that <em>some</em> court would have adopted it.  The Court does not even point to a <em>post</em>-<em>McNally</em> case that reads 1346 to cover only bribery and kickbacks, and I am aware of none.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The canon of constitutional avoidance, on which the Court so heavily relies, see <em>ante</em>, at 41-42, states that "when the constitutionality of a statute is assailed, if the statute be reasonably susceptible of two interpretations, by one of which it would be unconstitutional and by the other valid, it is our plain duty to adopt that construction which will save the statute from constitutional infirmity."  <em>United States ex rel. Attorney General</em> v. <em>Delaware &amp; Hudson Co.</em>, 213 U.&nbsp;S. 366, 407 (1909); see also <em>United States</em> v. <em>Rumely</em>, 345 U.&nbsp;S. 41, 45 (1953) (describing the canon as decisive "in the choice of fair alternatives").  Here there is no choice to be made between two "fair alternatives."  Until today, no one has thought (and there is no basis for thinking) that the honest-services statute prohibited only bribery and kickbacks.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I certainly agree with the Court that we must, "if we can," uphold, rather than "condemn," Congress's enactments, <em>ante</em>, at 38.  But I do not believe we have the power, in order to uphold an enactment, to rewrite it.  Congress enacted the entirety of the pre-<em>McNally </em>honest-services law, the content of which is (to put it mildly) unclear.  In prior vagueness cases, we have resisted the temptation to make all things right with the stroke of our pen.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Smith</em> v. <em>Goguen</em>, 415 U.&nbsp;S. 566, 575 (1974).  I would show the same restraint today, and reverse Skilling's conviction on the basis that 1346 provides no "ascertainable standard" for the conduct it condemns, <em>L. Cohen</em>, 255 U.&nbsp;S., at 89.  Instead, the Court today adds to our functions the prescription of criminal law.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A brief word about the appropriate remedy.  As I noted <em>supra</em>, at 2, Skilling has argued that 1346 cannot be constitutionally applied to him because it affords no definition of the right whose deprivation it prohibits.  Though this reasoning is categorical, it does not make Skilling's challenge a "facial" one, in the sense that it seeks invalidation of the statute in all its applications, as opposed to preventing its enforcement against him.  I continue to doubt whether "striking down" a statute is ever an appropriate exercise of our Article III power.  See <em>Chicago</em> v. <em>Morales</em>, 527 U.&nbsp;S. 41, 77 (1999) (S<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>calia</small></span>, J., dissenting).  In the present case, the universality of the infirmity Skilling identifies in 1346 may mean that if he wins, anyone else prosecuted under the statute will win as well, see <em>Smith</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 576-578.  But Skilling only asks that <em>his</em> conviction be reversed, Brief for Petitioner 57-58, so <em>the remedy</em> he seeks is not facial invalidation.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I would therefore reverse Skilling's conviction under 1346 on the ground that it fails to define the conduct it prohibits.  The fate of the statute in future prosecutions--obvious from my reasoning in the case--would be a matter for <em>stare decisis</em>.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">***</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is hard to imagine a case that more clearly fits the description of what Chief Justice Waite said could not be done, in a colorful passage oft-cited in our vagueness opinions, <em>United States </em>v. <em>Reese</em>, 92 U.&nbsp;S., at 221:</p>
<blockquote><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The question, then, to be determined, is, whether we can introduce words of limitation into a penal statute so as to make it specific, when, as expressed, it is general only.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"It would certainly be dangerous if the legislature could set a net large enough to catch all possible offenders, and leave it to the courts to step inside and say who could be rightfully detained, and who should be set at large.  This would, to some extent, substitute the judicial for the legislative department of the government...&nbsp;.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"To limit this statute in the manner now asked for would be to make a new law, not to enforce an old one.  This is no part of our duty."</p>
</blockquote><p></p><hr>

<a name="concurrence2"></a>



<center><h2><p align="CENTER">JEFFREY K. SKILLING, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> UNITED STATES</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER">on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the fifth circuit</p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[June 24, 2010]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span>, concurring in part and concurring in the judgment.</p>
<p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I join the judgment of the Court and all but Part II of the Court's opinion.  I write separately to address petitioner's jury-trial argument.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Sixth Amendment guarantees criminal defendants a trial before "an impartial jury."  In my view, this requirement is satisfied so long as no biased juror is actually seated at trial.  Of course, evidence of pretrial media attention and widespread community hostility may play a role in the bias inquiry.  Such evidence may be important in assessing the adequacy of <em>voir dire</em>, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Mu'Min</em> v. <em>Virginia</em>, 500 U.&nbsp;S. 415, 428-432 (1991), or in reviewing the denial of requests to dismiss particular jurors for cause, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Patton</em> v. <em>Yount</em>, 467 U.&nbsp;S. 1025, 1036-1040 (1984).  There are occasions in which such evidence weighs heavily in favor of a change of venue.  In the end, however, if no biased jury is actually seated, there is no violation of the defendant's right to an impartial jury.  See <em>id</em>., at 1031-1035, 1041; <em>Murphy</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 421 U.&nbsp;S. 794, 800-801, 803 (1975); see also <em>Rivera</em> v. <em>Illinois</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2009) (slip op., at 7-8); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Martinez-Salazar</em>, 528 U.&nbsp;S. 304, 311, 316-317 (2000); <em>Smith</em> v. <em>Phillips</em>, 455 U.&nbsp;S. 209, 215-218 (1982).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Petitioner advances a very different understanding of the jury-trial right.  Where there is extraordinary pretrial publicity and community hostility, he contends, a court must presume juror prejudice and thus grant a change of venue.  Brief for Petitioner 25-34.  I disagree.  Careful <em>voir dire</em> can often ensure the selection of impartial jurors even where pretrial media coverage has generated much hostile community sentiment.  Moreover, once a jury has been selected, there are measures that a trial judge may take to insulate jurors from media coverage during the course of the trial.  What the Sixth Amendment requires is "an impartial jury."  If the jury that sits and returns a verdict is impartial, a defendant has received what the Sixth Amendment requires.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The rule that petitioner advances departs from the text of the Sixth Amendment and is difficult to apply.  It requires a trial judge to determine whether the adverse pretrial media coverage and community hostility in a particular case has reached a certain level of severity, but there is no clear way of demarcating that level or of determining whether it has been met.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Petitioner relies chiefly on three cases from the 1960's--<em>Sheppard</em> v. <em>Maxwell</em>, 384 U.&nbsp;S. 333 (1966), <em>Estes</em> v. <em>Texas</em>, 381 U.&nbsp;S. 532 (1965), and <em>Rideau</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 373 U.&nbsp;S. 723 (1963).  I do not read those cases as demanding petitioner's suggested approach.  As the Court notes, <em>Sheppard </em>and <em>Estes</em> primarily "involved media interference with courtroom proceedings <em>during</em> trial."  <em>Ante</em>, at 16, n.&nbsp;14; see also <em>post</em>, at 20 (S<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>otomayor</small></span>, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part).  <em>Rideau</em> involved unique events in a small community.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I share some of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor'</small></span>s concerns about the adequacy of the <em>voir dire</em> in this case and the trial judge's findings that certain jurors could be impartial.  See <em>post</em>, at 34-37.  But those highly fact-specific issues are not within the question presented.  Pet. for Cert. i.  I also do not understand the opinion of the Court as reaching any question regarding a change of venue under Federal Rule of Criminal Procedure 21.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Because petitioner, in my view, is not entitled to a reversal of the decision below on the jury-trial question that is before us, I join the judgment of the Court in full.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<a name="other1"></a>



<center><h2><p align="CENTER">JEFFREY K. SKILLING, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> UNITED STATES</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER">on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the fifth circuit</p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[June 24, 2010]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor</small></span>, with whom <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Stevens </small></span>and<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> Justice Breyer</small></span> join, concurring in part and dissenting in part.</p>
<p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I concur in the Court's resolution of the honest-services fraud question and join Part III of its opinion.  I respectfully dissent, however, from the Court's conclusion that Jeffrey Skilling received a fair trial before an impartial jury.  Under our relevant precedents, the more intense the public's antipathy toward a defendant, the more careful a court must be to prevent that sentiment from tainting the jury.  In this case, passions ran extremely high.  The sudden collapse of Enron directly affected thousands of people in the Houston area and shocked the entire community.  The accompanying barrage of local media coverage was massive in volume and often caustic in tone.  As Enron's one-time CEO, Skilling was at the center of the storm.  Even if these extraordinary circumstances did not constitutionally compel a change of venue, they required the District Court to conduct a thorough <em>voir dire </em>in which prospective jurors' attitudes about the case were closely scrutinized.  The District Court's inquiry lacked the necessary thoroughness and left serious doubts about whether the jury empaneled to decide Skilling's case was capable of rendering an impartial decision based solely on the evidence presented in the courtroom.  Accordingly, I would grant Skilling relief on his fair-trial claim.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">I</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority understates the breadth and depth of community hostility toward Skilling and overlooks significant deficiencies in the District Court's jury selection process.  The failure of Enron wounded Houston deeply.  Virtually overnight, what had been the city's "largest, most visible, and most prosperous company," its "foremost social and charitable force," and "a source of civic pride" was reduced to a "shattered shell."  App. 11, 13, pp. 649a-650a, 1152a.  Thousands of the company's employees lost their jobs and saw their retirement savings vanish.  As the effects rippled through the local economy, thousands of additional jobs disappeared, businesses shuttered, and community groups that once benefited from Enron's largesse felt the loss of millions of dollars in contributions.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, 3 Supp. Record 1229, 1267; see also 554 F.&nbsp;3d 529, 560 (CA5 2009) ("Accounting firms that serviced Enron's books had less work, hotels had more open rooms, restaurants sold fewer meals, and so on").  Enron's community ties were so extensive that the entire local U.&nbsp;S. Attorney's Office was forced to recuse itself from the Government's investigation into the company's fall.  See 3 Supp. Record 608 (official press release).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;With Enron's demise affecting the lives of so many Houstonians, local media coverage of the story saturated the community.  According to a defense media expert, the Houston Chronicle--the area's leading newspaper--assigned as many as 12 reporters to work on the Enron story full time.  App. 568a-569a.  The paper<em> </em>mentioned Enron in more than 4,000 articles during the 3-year period following the company's December 2001 bankruptcy filing.  Hundreds of these articles discussed Skilling by name.  See 3 Supp. Record 2114.  Skilling's expert, a professional journalist and academic with 30 years' experience, could not "recall another instance where a local paper dedicated as many resources to a single topic over such an extended period of time as the Houston Chronicle ... dedicated to Enron."  App. 32, p. 570a.  Local television news coverage was similarly pervasive and, in terms of "editorial theme," "largely followed the Chronicle's lead."  <em>Id</em>., 11, at 559a; see also <em>id</em>., at 717a.  Between May 2002 and October 2004, local stations aired an estimated 19,000 news segments involving Enron, more than 1600 of which mentioned Skilling.  3 Supp. Record 2116.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;While many of the stories were straightforward news items, many others conveyed and amplified the community's outrage at the top executives perceived to be responsible for the company's bankruptcy.  A Chronicle<em> </em>report on Skilling's 2002 testimony before Congress is typical of the coverage.  It began, "Across Houston, Enron employees watched former chief executive Jeffrey Skilling's congressional testimony on television, turning incredulous, angry and then sarcastic by turns, as a man they knew as savvy and detail-oriented pleaded memory failure and ignorance about critical financial transactions at the now-collapsed energy giant."  App. 1218a.  "&nbsp;'He is lying; he knew everything,' said [an employee], who said she had seen Skilling frequently over her 18 years with the firm, where Skilling was known for his intimate grasp of the inner doings at the company.  'I am getting sicker by the minute.'&nbsp;"  <em>Id</em>., at 1219a.  A companion piece quoted a local attorney who called Skilling an "idiot" who was "in denial"; he added, "I'm glad [Skilling's] not my client."  <em>Id</em>., at 592a-593a.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Articles deriding Enron's senior executives were juxtaposed with pieces expressing sympathy toward and solidarity with the company's many victims.  Skilling's media expert counted nearly a hundred victim-related stories in the Chronicle, including a "multi-page layout entitled 'The Faces of Enron,'&nbsp;" which poignantly described the gut-wrenching experiences of former employees who lost vast sums of money, faced eviction from their homes, could not afford Christmas gifts for their children, and felt "scared," "hurt," "humiliat[ed]," "helpless," and "betrayed."  <em>Id</em>., 71, at 585a-586a.  The conventional wisdom that blame for Enron's devastating implosion and the ensuing human tragedy ultimately rested with Skilling and former Enron Chairman Kenneth Lay became so deeply ingrained in the popular imagination that references to their involvement even turned up on the sports pages: "If you believe the story about [Coach Bill Parcells] not having anything to do with the end of Emmitt Smith's Cowboys career, then you probably believe in other far-fetched concepts.  Like Jeff Skilling having nothing to do with Enron's collapse."  3 Supp. Record 811.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When a federal grand jury indicted Skilling, Lay, and Richard Causey--Enron's former chief accounting officer--in 2004 on charges of conspiracy to defraud, securities fraud, and other crimes, the media placed them directly in its crosshairs.  In the words of one article, "there was one thing those whose lives were touched by the once-exalted company all seemed to agree upon: The indictment of former Enron CEO Jeff Skilling was overdue."  App. 1393a.  Scoffing at Skilling's attempts to paint himself as "a 'victim' of his subordinates," <em>id</em>., at 1394a, the Chronicle<em> </em>derided "the doofus defense" that Lay and Skilling were expected to offer, <em>id</em>., at 1401a.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 1" name="FRother1.1" href="#FNother1.1">1</a></sup>  The Chronicle<em> </em>referred to the coming Skilling/Lay trial as "the main event" and "The Big One," which would finally bring "the true measure of justice in the Enron saga."  Record 40002; App. 1457a, 1460a.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 2" name="FRother1.2" href="#FNother1.2">2</a></sup>  On the day the superseding indictment charging Lay was issued, "the Chronicle dedicated three-quarters of its front page, 2 other full pages, and substantial portions of 4 other pages, all in the front or business sections, to th[e] story."  <em>Id</em>., 57, at<em> </em>580a-581a.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Citing the widely felt sense of victimhood among Houstonians and the voluminous adverse publicity, Skilling moved in November 2004 for a change of venue.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 3" name="FRother1.3" href="#FNother1.3">3</a></sup>  The District Court denied the motion, characterizing the media coverage as largely "objective and unemotional."  App. to Brief for United States 11a.  <em>Voir dire</em>, it concluded, would provide an effective means to "ferret out any bias" in the jury pool.  <em>Id.</em>, at 18a; see <em>ante</em>, at 4.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To that end, the District Court began the jury selection process by mailing screening questionnaires to 400 prospective jurors in November 2005.  The completed questionnaires of the 283 respondents not excused for hardship dramatically illustrated the widespread impact of Enron's collapse on the Houston community and confirmed the intense animosity of Houstonians toward Skilling and his codefendants.  More than one-third of the prospective jurors (approximately 99 of 283, by my count) indicated that they or persons they knew had lost money or jobs as a result of the Enron bankruptcy.  Two-thirds of the jurors (about 188 of 283) expressed views about Enron or the defendants that suggested a potential predisposition to convict.  In many instances, they did not mince words, describing Skilling as "smug," "arrogant," "brash," "conceited," "greedy," "deceitful," "totally unethical and criminal," "a crook," "the biggest liar on the face of the earth," and "guilty as sin" (capitalization omitted).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 4" name="FRother1.4" href="#FNother1.4">4</a></sup>  Only about 5 percent of the prospective jurors (15 of 283) did not read the Houston Chronicle, had not otherwise "heard or read about any of the Enron cases," Record 13019, were not connected to Enron victims, and gave no answers suggesting possible antipathy toward the defendants.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 5" name="FRother1.5" href="#FNother1.5">5</a></sup>  The parties jointly stipulated to the dismissal of 119 members of the jury pool for cause, hardship, or disability, but numerous individuals who had made harsh comments about Skilling remained.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 6" name="FRother1.6" href="#FNother1.6">6</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;On December 28, 2005, shortly after the questionnaires had been returned, Causey pleaded guilty.  The plea was covered in lead newspaper and television stories.  A front-page headline in the Chronicle<em> </em>proclaimed that "Causey's plea wreaks havoc for Lay, Skilling."  Record 12049, n. 13; see also <em>ibid</em>. (quoting a former U.&nbsp;S. attorney who described the plea as "a serious blow to the defense").  A Chronicle<em> </em>editorial opined that "Causey's admission of securities fraud ... . makes less plausible Lay's claim that most of the guilty pleas were the result of prosecutorial pressure rather than actual wrongdoing."  <em>Id.</em>, at 12391.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;With the trial date quickly approaching, Skilling renewed his change-of-venue motion, arguing that both the questionnaire responses and the Causey guilty plea confirmed that he could not receive a fair trial in Houston.  In the alternative, Skilling asserted that "defendants are entitled to a more thorough jury selection process than currently envisioned by the [c]ourt."  <em>Id.</em>, at 12067.  The court had announced its intention to question individual jurors at the bench with one attorney for each side present, and to complete the <em>voir dire </em>in a single day.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>id</em>., at 11804-11805, 11808.  Skilling proposed, <em>inter alia</em>, that defense counsel be afforded a greater role in questioning, <em>id</em>., at 12074; that jurors be questioned privately <em>in</em> <em>camera </em>or in a closed courtroom where it would be easier for counsel to consult with their colleagues, clients, and jury consultants, <em>id</em>., at 12070-12072; and that the court "avoid leading questions," which "tend to [e]licit affirmative responses from prospective jurors that may not reflect their actual views,"<em> id</em>., at 12072.  At a minimum, Skilling asserted, the court should grant a continuance of at least 30 days and send a revised questionnaire to a new group of prospective jurors.  <em>Id</em>., at 12074-12075.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The District Court denied Skilling's motion without a hearing, stating in a brief order that it was "not persuaded that the evidence or arguments urged by defendants ... establish that pretrial publicity and/or community prejudice raise a presumption of inherent jury prejudice."  <em>Id</em>., at 14115.  According to the court, the "jury questionnaires sent to the remaining members of the jury panel and the court's voir dire examination of the jury panel provide adequate safeguards to defendants and will result in the selection of a fair and impartial jury in this case."  <em>Id</em>., at 14115-14116.  The court did agree to delay the trial by two weeks, until January 30, 2006.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The coming trial featured prominently in local news outlets.  A front-page, eve-of-trial story in the Chronicle<em> </em>described "the hurt and anger and resentment" that had been "churn[ing] inside" Houstonians since Enron's collapse.  <em>Id.</em>, at 39946.  Again criticizing Lay and Skilling for offering a "doofus defense" ("a plea of not guilty by reason of empty-headedness"), the paper stated that "Lay and Skilling took hundreds of millions in compensation yet now fail to accept the responsibility that went with it."  <em>Ibid</em>.  The article allowed that the defendants' guilt, "though perhaps widely assumed, remains even now an assertion.  A jury now takes up the task of deciding whether that assertion is valid."  <em>Id</em>., at 39947.  The next paragraph, however, assured readers that "it's normal for your skin to crawl when Lay or Skilling claim with doe-eyed innocence that they were unaware that something was amiss at Enron.  The company's utter failure belies the claim."  <em>Ibid</em>. (one paragraph break omitted); see also <em>id</em>., at 39904 (declaring that Lay and Skilling would "have to offer a convincing explanation for how executives once touted as corporate geniuses could be so much in the dark about the illegal activities and deceptive finances of their own company").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is against this backdrop of widespread community impact and pervasive pretrial publicity that jury selection in Skilling's case unfolded.  Approximately 160 prospective jurors appeared for <em>voir dire </em>at a federal courthouse located "about six blocks from Enron's former headquarters."  554 F.&nbsp;3d, at 561.  Addressing them as a group, the District Court began by briefly describing the case and providing a standard admonition about the need to be fair and impartial and to decide the case based solely on the trial evidence and jury instructions.  The court then asked whether anyone had "any reservations about your ability to conscientiously and fairly follow these very important rules."  App. 815a.  Two individuals raised their hands and were called forward to the bench.  One told the court that he thought Lay and Skilling "knew exactly what they were doing" and would have to prove their innocence.  <em>Id</em>., at 818a-819a.  The second juror, who had stated on his written questionnaire that he held no opinion that would preclude him from being impartial, declared that he "would dearly love to sit on this jury.  I would love to claim responsibility, at least 1/12 of the responsibility, for putting these sons of bitches away for the rest of their lives."  <em>Id</em>., at 819a-820a.  The court excused both jurors for cause.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The court proceeded to question individual jurors from the bench.  As the majority recounts, <em>ante</em>, at 7-8, the court asked them a few general yes/no questions about their exposure to Enron-related news, often variations of, "Do you recall any particular articles that stand out that you've read about the case?"  App. 850a.  The court also asked about questionnaire answers that suggested bias, focusing mainly on whether, notwithstanding seemingly partial comments, the prospective jurors believed they "could be fair" and "put the government to its proof."  <em>Id</em>., at 852a.  Counsel were permitted to follow up on issues raised by the court.  The court made clear, however, that its patience would be limited, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>id.</em>, at 879a, and questioning tended to be brief--generally less than five minutes per person.  Even so, it exposed disqualifying biases among several prospective jurors who had earlier expressed no concerns about their ability to be fair.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 7" name="FRother1.7" href="#FNother1.7">7</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Once it identified 38 qualified prospective jurors, the court allowed the defense and Government to exercise their allotted peremptory challenges.
