Indeed, the "use [of] a peremptory challenge to effect an instantaneous cure of the error" exemplifies "a principal reason for peremptories: to help secure the constitutional guarantee of trial by an impartial jury."  <em>Id.,</em> at 316.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 33" name="FNopinion1.33" href="#FRopinion1.33">33</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Skilling's trial counsel and jury consultants apparently did not regard Juror 11 as so "obvious[ly] bias[ed]," Brief for Petitioner 35, as to warrant exercise of a peremptory challenge.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 34" name="FNopinion1.34" href="#FRopinion1.34">34</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although Skilling raised no objection to Juror 10 and Juror 87 at trial, his briefs in this Court impugn their impartiality.  Brief for Petitioner 14-15; Reply Brief 13.  Even if we allowed these tardy pleas, the <em>voir dire</em> testimony of the two jurors gives sufficient assurance that they were unbiased.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> App. 850a-853a (Juror 10) (did not prejudge Skilling's guilt, indicated he could follow the court's instructions and make the Government prove its case, stated he could be fair to Skilling, and said he would "judge on the facts"); <em>id.,</em> at 974a (Juror 87) (had "not formed an opinion" on whether Skilling was guilty and affirmed she could adhere to the presumption of innocence).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNopinion1.35" href="#FRopinion1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Our decisions have rightly set a high bar for allegations of juror prejudice due to pretrial publicity.  See, <em>e.g., Mu'Min</em>, 500 U.&nbsp;S. 415; <em>Patton</em> v. <em>Yount</em>, 467 U.&nbsp;S. 1025 (1984); <em>Murphy</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 421 U.&nbsp;S. 794 (1975).  News coverage of civil and criminal trials of public interest conveys to society at large how our justice system operates.  And it is a premise of that system that jurors will set aside their preconceptions when they enter the courtroom and decide cases based on the evidence presented.  Trial judges generally take care so to instruct jurors, and the District Court did just that in this case.  App. 1026a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 36" name="FNopinion1.36" href="#FRopinion1.36">36</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In addition to upholding honest-services prosecutions, courts also increasingly approved use of the mail-fraud statute to attack corruption that deprived victims of other kinds of intangible rights, including election fraud and privacy violations.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Cleveland</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 531 U.&nbsp;S. 12, 18, n. 2 (2000); <em>McNally</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S. 350, 362-364, and nn. 1-4 (1987) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Stevens</small></span>, J., dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 37" name="FNopinion1.37" href="#FRopinion1.37">37</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Courts have disagreed about whether 1346 prosecutions must be based on a violation of state law, compare, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Brumley</em>, 116 F.&nbsp;3d 728, 734-735 (CA5 1997) (en banc), with, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States </em>v. <em>Weyhrauch</em>, 548 F.&nbsp;3d 1237, 1245-1246 (CA9 2008), vacated and remanded, <em>post</em>, p. ___; whether a defendant must contemplate that the victim suffer economic harm, compare, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Sun-Diamond Growers of Cal.</em>, 138 F.&nbsp;3d 961, 973 (CADC 1998), with, <em>e.g., United States</em> v. <em>Black</em>, 530 F.&nbsp;3d 596, 600-602 (CA7 2008), vacated and remanded, <em>post</em>, p. ___; and whether the defendant must act in pursuit of private gain, compare, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Bloom</em>, 149 F.&nbsp;3d 649, 655 (CA7 1998), with, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Panarella</em>, 277 F.&nbsp;3d 678, 692 (CA3 2002). </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 38" name="FNopinion1.38" href="#FRopinion1.38">38</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Rybicki</em>, 354 F.&nbsp;3d 124, 132 (CA2 2003) (en banc); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Hausmann</em>, 345 F.&nbsp;3d 952, 958 (CA7 2003); <em>United States </em>v. <em>Welch</em>, 327 F.&nbsp;3d 1081, 1109, n. 29 (CA10 2003); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Frega</em>, 179 F.&nbsp;3d 793, 803 (CA9 1999); <em>Brumley</em>, 116 F.&nbsp;3d, at 732-733; <em>United States </em>v. <em>Frost</em>, 125 F.&nbsp;3d 346, 370-372 (CA6 1997); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Waymer</em>, 55 F.&nbsp;3d 564, 568-569 (CA11 1995); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Bryan</em>, 58 F.&nbsp;3d 933, 941 (CA4 1995).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 39" name="FNopinion1.39" href="#FRopinion1.39">39</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although verbal formulations varied slightly, the words employed by the Courts of Appeals prior to <em>McNally </em>described the same concept: "honest services," <em>e.g., United States</em> v. <em>Bruno</em>, 809 F.&nbsp;2d 1097, 1105 (CA5 1987); "honest and faithful services," <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Brown</em>, 540 F.&nbsp;2d 364, 374 (CA8 1976); and "faithful and honest services," <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Diggs</em>, 613 F.&nbsp;2d 988, 998 (CADC 1979).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 40" name="FNopinion1.40" href="#FRopinion1.40">40</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We considered a similar Court-Congress interplay in <em>McDermott Int'l, Inc.</em> v. <em>Wilander</em>, 498 U.&nbsp;S. 337 (1991), which involved the interpretation of the term "seaman" in the Jones Act, 46 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. App. 688 (2000 ed.).  The Act, we recognized, "respond[ed] directly to" our decision in <em>The Osceola</em>, 189 U.&nbsp;S. 158 (1903), and "adopt[ed] without further elaboration the term used in" that case, so we "assume[d] that the Jones Act use[d] 'seaman' in the same way."  498 U.&nbsp;S., at 342.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 41" name="FNopinion1.41" href="#FRopinion1.41">41</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"This cardinal principle has its roots in Chief Justice Marshall's opinion for the Court in <em>Murray</em> v. <em>The Charming Betsy</em>, 2 Cranch 64, 118 (1804), and has for so long been applied by this Court that it is beyond debate."  <em>Edward J. DeBartolo Corp.</em> v. <em>Florida Gulf Coast Building &amp; Constr. Trades Council</em>, 485 U.&nbsp;S. 568, 575 (1988).  See, <em>e.g., New York</em> v. <em>Ferber</em>, 458 U.&nbsp;S. 747, 769, n. 24 (1982); <em>NLRB</em> v. <em>Catholic Bishop of Chicago</em>, 440 U.&nbsp;S. 490, 500-501 (1979); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Thirty-seven Photographs</em>, 402 U.&nbsp;S. 363, 368-370 (1971); <em>Machinists</em> v. <em>Street</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 740, 749-750 (1961); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Rumely</em>, 345 U.&nbsp;S. 41, 45 (1953); <em>Winters</em> v. <em>New York</em>, 333 U.&nbsp;S. 507, 517 (1948); <em>Crowell</em> v. <em>Benson</em>, 285 U.&nbsp;S. 22, 62 (1932); <em>Lucas</em> v. <em>Alexander</em>, 279 U.&nbsp;S. 573, 577 (1929); <em>Richmond Screw Anchor Co.</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 275 U.&nbsp;S. 331, 346 (1928); <em>Panama R. Co.</em> v. <em>Johnson</em>, 264 U.&nbsp;S. 375, 390 (1924); <em>United States ex rel. Attorney General</em> v. <em>Delaware &amp; Hudson Co.</em>, 213 U.&nbsp;S. 366, 407-408 (1909); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Coombs</em>, 12 Pet. 72, 76 (1838) (Story, J.); <em>Parsons</em> v. <em>Bedford</em>, 3 Pet. 433, 448-449 (1830) (Story, J.).  Cf. <em>Chaplinsky</em> v. <em>New Hampshire</em>, 315 U.&nbsp;S. 568, 573 (1942) (statute made it criminal to address "any offensive, derisive, or annoying word" to any person in a public place; vagueness obviated by state-court construction of the statute to cover only words having "a direct tendency to cause acts of violence" by the addressee (internal quotation marks omitted)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 42" name="FNopinion1.42" href="#FRopinion1.42">42</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> emphasizes divisions in the Courts of Appeals regarding the source and scope of fiduciary duties.  <em>Post</em>, at 3-5.  But these debates were rare in bribe and kickback cases.  The existence of a fiduciary relationship, under any definition of that term, was usually beyond dispute; examples include public official-public, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Mandel</em>, 591 F.&nbsp;2d 1347 (CA4 1979); employee-employer, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States</em> v. <em>Bohonus</em>, 628 F.&nbsp;2d 1167 (CA9 1980); and union official-union members, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>United States </em>v. <em>Price</em>, 788 F.&nbsp;2d 234 (CA4 1986).  See generally <em>Chiarella</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 445 U.&nbsp;S. 222, 233 (1980) (noting the "established doctrine that [a fiduciary] duty arises from a specific relationship between two parties").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 43" name="FNopinion1.43" href="#FRopinion1.43">43</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Apprised that a broader reading of 1346 could render the statute impermissibly vague, Congress, we believe, would have drawn the honest-services line, as we do now, at bribery and kickback schemes.  Cf. <em>Levin</em> v. <em>Commerce Energy, Inc.</em>, 560 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2010) (slip op., at 11) ("[C]ourts may attempt ... to implement what the legislature would have willed had it been apprised of the constitutional infirmity."); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Booker</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S. 220, 246 (2005) ("We seek to determine what 'Congress would have intended' in light of the Court's constitutional holding.").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 44" name="FNopinion1.44" href="#FRopinion1.44">44</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia </small></span>charges that our construction of 1346 is "not interpretation but invention."  <em>Post</em>, at 8.  Stating that he "know[s] of no precedent for ... 'paring down'&nbsp;" the pre-<em>McNally</em> case law to its core, <em>ibid.</em>, he contends that the Court today "wield[s] a power we long ago abjured: the power to define new federal crimes," <em>post</em>, at 1.  See also, <em>e.g., post</em>, at 9, 10, 11.  As noted <em>supra</em>, at 41-42, and n. 41, cases "paring down" federal statutes to avoid constitutional shoals are legion.  These cases recognize that the Court does not <em>legislate</em>, but instead <em>respects the legislature</em>, by preserving a statute through a limiting interpretation.  See <em>United States</em> v. <em>Lanier</em>, 520 U.&nbsp;S. 259, 267-268, n.&nbsp;6 (1997) (This Court does not "create a common law crime" by adopting a "narrow[ing] constru[ction]." (internal quotation marks omitted)); <em>supra</em>, at 44-45, n. 43.  Given that the Courts of Appeals uniformly recognized bribery and kickback schemes as honest-services fraud before <em>McNally</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S. 350, and that these schemes composed the lion's share of honest-services cases, limiting 1346 to these heartland applications is surely "fairly possible."  <em>Boos</em> v. <em>Barry</em>, 485 U.&nbsp;S. 312, 331 (1988); cf. <em>Clark</em> v. <em>Martinez</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S. 371, 380 (2005) (opinion of the Court by <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia, J.</small></span>) (when adopting a limiting construction, "[t]he lowest common denominator, as it were, must govern").  So construed, the statute is not unconstitutionally vague.  See <em>infra</em>, at 48-49; <em>post</em>, at 8.  Only by taking a wrecking ball to a statute that can be salvaged through a reasonable narrowing interpretation would we act out of step with precedent.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 45" name="FNopinion1.45" href="#FRopinion1.45">45</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;If Congress were to take up the enterprise of criminalizing "undisclosed self-dealing by a public official or private employee," Brief for United States 43, it would have to employ standards of sufficient definiteness and specificity to overcome due process concerns.  The Government proposes a standard that prohibits the "taking of official action by the employee that furthers his own undisclosed financial interests while purporting to act in the interests of those to whom he owes a fiduciary duty," so long as the employee acts with a specific intent to deceive and the undisclosed conduct could influence the victim to change its behavior.  <em>Id</em>., at 43-44.  See also <em>id</em>., at 40-41.  That formulation, however, leaves many questions unanswered.  How direct or significant does the conflicting financial interest have to be?  To what extent does the official action have to further that interest in order to amount to fraud?  To whom should the disclosure be made and what information should it convey?  These questions and others call for particular care in attempting to formulate an adequate criminal prohibition in this context.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 46" name="FNopinion1.46" href="#FRopinion1.46">46</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Overlap with other federal statutes does not render 1346 superfluous.  The principal federal bribery statute, 201, for example, generally applies only to federal public officials, so 1346's application to state and local corruption and to private-sector fraud reaches misconduct that might otherwise go unpunished.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 47" name="FNopinion1.47" href="#FRopinion1.47">47</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Fifth Circuit appeared to prejudge this issue, noting that, "if any of the three objects of Skilling's conspiracy offers a legally insufficient theory," it "must set aside his conviction."  554 F.&nbsp;3d, at 543.  That reasoning relied on the mistaken premise that <em>Hedgpeth</em> v. <em>Pulido</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2008) <em>(per curiam)</em>, governs only cases on collateral review.  See 554 F.&nbsp;3d, at 543, n.&nbsp;10.  Harmless-error analysis, we clarify, applies equally to cases on direct appeal.  Accordingly, the Fifth Circuit, on remand, should take a fresh look at the parties' harmless-error arguments.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNconcurrence1.1" href="#FRconcurrence1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court is untroubled by these divisions because "these debates were rare in bribe and kickback cases," in which "[t]he existence of a fiduciary relationship, under any definition of that term, was usually beyond dispute," <em>ante</em>, at 43, n. 42.  This misses the point.  The Courts of Appeals may have consistently found unlawful the acceptance of a bribe or kickback by one or another sort of fiduciary, but they have not consistently described (as the statute does not) any test for who is a fiduciary.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNconcurrence1.2" href="#FRconcurrence1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Courts since 1346's enactment have fared no better, reproducing some of the same disputes that predated <em>McNally</em>.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Sorich</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2009) (S<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>calia</small></span>, J., dissenting from denial of certiorari) (slip op., at 3-4) (collecting cases).  We have previously found important to our vagueness analysis "the conflicting results which have arisen from the painstaking attempts of enlightened judges in seeking to carry out [a] statute in cases brought before them."  <em>United States</em> v. <em>L. Cohen Grocery Co.</em>, 255 U.&nbsp;S. 81, 89 (1921).  I am at a loss to explain why the Court barely mentions those conflicts today.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNconcurrence1.3" href="#FRconcurrence1.3">3</a></strong><br><p> The only alleged precedent the Court dares to describe is <em>Civil Service Comm'n</em> v. <em>Letter Carriers</em>, 413 U.&nbsp;S. 548 (1973).  That case involved a provision of the Hatch Act incorporating prior adjudications of the Civil Service Commission.  We upheld the provision against a vagueness challenge--not, however, by "paring down" the adjudications to a more narrow rule that we invented, but by concluding that what they held was not vague.  See <em>id.</em>, at 571-574.  The string of cases the Court lists, see <em>ante</em>, at 41-42, n. 41, (almost none of which addressed claims of vagueness), have nothing to do with "paring down."  The one that comes closest, <em>United States </em>v. <em>Thirty-seven Photographs</em>, 402 U.&nbsp;S. 363 (1971), specified a time limit within which proceedings authorized by statute for the forfeiture of obscene imported materials had to be commenced and completed.  That is not much different from "reading in" a reasonable-time requirement for obligations undertaken in contracts, and can hardly be described as a rewriting or "paring down" of the statute.  The Court relied on legislative history anticipating that the proceedings would be prompt, <em>id.</em>, at 370-371, and noted that (unlike here) it was not "decid[ing] issues of policy," <em>id.</em>, at 372.</p>

A court will decide the particulars, but yes, Ken Lay knew"); <em>id</em>., 1406a, 1409a (describing Enron's collapse as "failure as a result of fraud" and criticizing Skilling for using "vitriol [as] a smokescreen" and "bolting for the door" just before Enron's stock price plummeted); 3 Supp. Record 1711 (discussing the role of Skilling and Lay in "the granddaddy of all corporate frauds").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNother1.8" href="#FRother1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Of course, even if the jury selection process is adequate, a trial court violates a defendant's right to an impartial jury if it erroneously denies a for-cause challenge to a biased venire member who ultimately sits on the jury.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Martinez-Salazar</em>, 528 U.&nbsp;S. 304, 316 (2000) ("[T]he seating of any juror who should have been dismissed for cause ... would require reversal").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNother1.9" href="#FRother1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Whether the District Court abused its discretion in declining to change venue pursuant to the Federal Rules of Criminal Procedure is a different question.  See Fed. Rule Crim. Proc. 21(a) ("Upon the defendant's motion, the court must transfer the proceeding against that defendant to another district if the court is satisfied that so great a prejudice against the defendant exists in the transferring district that the defendant cannot obtain a fair and impartial trial there").  As this Court has indicated, its supervisory powers confer "more latitude" to set standards for the conduct of trials in federal courts than in state courts.  <em>Mu'Min</em> v. <em>Virginia</em>, 500 U.&nbsp;S. 415, 424 (1991).  While the circumstances may not constitutionally compel a change of venue "without pausing to examine ... the <em>voir dire</em>," <em>Rideau</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 373 U.&nbsp;S. 723, 727 (1963), the widely felt sense of victimhood among Houstonians and the community's deep-seated animus toward Skilling certainly meant that the task of reliably identifying untainted jurors posed a major challenge, with no guarantee of success.  It likely would have been far easier to empanel an impartial jury in a venue where the Enron story had less salience.  I thus agree with the Court of Appeals that "[i]t would not have been imprudent for the [District] [C]ourt to have granted Skilling's transfer motion."  554 F.&nbsp;3d 529, 558 (CA5 2009).  Skilling, however, likely forfeited any Rule 21 or supervisory powers claim by failing to present it either in his opening brief before the Fifth Circuit, see <em>id</em>., at 559, n. 39, or in his petition for certiorari, cf. <em>ante</em>, at 12, n.&nbsp;11.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNother1.10" href="#FRother1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;One of Skilling's experts noted that, "[i]n cases involving 200 or more articles, trial judges granted a change of venue 59% of the time."  App. 30, p. 611a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNother1.11" href="#FRother1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, 554 F.&nbsp;3d, at 559, n. 42 ("I'm livid, absolutely livid ... . I have lost my entire friggin' retirement to these people.  They have raped all of us" (internal quotation marks omitted)); App. 382a ("Hurting that many elderly people so severely is, I feel, the equivalent of being an axe murderer.  His actions were just as harmful as an axe murderer to the [community]" (alteration in original)); <em>id</em>., at 1152a-1153a ("Not having the stuff of suicide bombers, Enron's executive pilots took full advantage of golden parachutes to bail out of their high-flying corporate jet after setting the craft on a course to financial oblivion.  In a business time frame, Enron pancaked faster than the twin towers"); <em>id</em>., at 1163a (noting that "Skilling's picture turned up alongside Osama bin Laden's on 'Wanted' posters inside the company headquarters").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNother1.12" href="#FRother1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Among the highlights: In 2002, Skilling testified before Congress, and other Enron executives invoked their Fifth Amendment rights; Enron auditor Arthur Andersen was indicted, tried, convicted, and sentenced on charges of obstruction of justice; the Enron Task Force charged Enron CFO and Skilling-protg Andrew Fastow with fraud, money laundering, and other crimes; and at least two Enron employees pleaded guilty on fraud and tax charges.  In 2003, the Enron Task Force indicted numerous Enron employees, including Ben Glisan, Jr. (the company's treasurer), Lea Fastow (wife of Andrew and an assistant treasurer), and more than half a dozen executives of Enron Broadband Services; several Enron employees entered guilty pleas and received prison sentences; and Enron filed its bankruptcy reorganization plan.  In 2004, Andrew and Lea Fastow both pleaded guilty; Skilling and Causey were indicted in February; a superseding indictment adding Lay was filed in July; a number of additional Enron employees entered guilty pleas; and former Enron employees and Merrill Lynch bankers were defendants in a 6-week trial in Houston concerning an Enron deal involving the sale of Nigerian barges.  In 2005, a 3-month trial was held in Houston for five executives of Enron Broadband Services; various pretrial proceedings occurred in the run up to the trial of Skilling, Lay, and Causey; and, three weeks before the scheduled trial date, Causey pleaded guilty to securities fraud.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNother1.13" href="#FRother1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority points out that the jury selection processes in the three previous Enron trials that had been held in Houston were similarly brief.  See <em>ante</em>, at 23.  The circumstances of those cases, however, were very different.  In particular, the defendants had not been personally subjected to anything approaching the withering public criticism that had been directed at Skilling and Lay.  As earlier noted, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 25-26, it was the trial of Skilling and Lay that was widely seen as the climactic event of the Enron saga.  Accordingly, my conclusion that the jury selection process in this unusual case did not suffice to select an impartial jury does not cast doubt on the adequacy of the processes used in the earlier Enron prosecutions.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Moreover, in referencing the length of the <em>voir dire</em> in this case, I do not mean to suggest that length should be a principal measure of the adequacy of a jury selection process.  Trial courts, including this one, should be commended for striving to be efficient, but they must always take care to ensure that their expeditiousness does not compromise a defendant's fair-trial right.  I also express no view with respect to court-led versus attorney-led <em>voir dire</em>.  Federal Rule of Criminal Procedure 24(a) gives district courts discretion to choose between these options, and I have no doubt that either is capable of producing an impartial jury even in high profile cases so long as the trial court assures that the scope of the <em>voir dire </em>is tailored to the circumstances.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNother1.14" href="#FRother1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Juror 33 brought up the plea in response to the District Court's question about whether he "recall[ed] listening to any particular programs about the case."  App. 888a.  Juror 96, meanwhile, told the court that he read the "whole" Houston Chronicle every day, including "all the articles about Enron."  <em>Id.</em>, at<em> </em>992a.  The court, however, did not ask any questions designed to elicit information about the Causey plea.  Instead, Juror 96 remarked on the plea only after Skilling's counsel managed to squeeze in a follow-up as to whether he had "read about any guilty pleas in this case over the last month or two."  <em>Id</em>., at 993a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNother1.15" href="#FRother1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Portions of the <em>voir dire </em>transcript erroneously refer to this prospective juror as "Juror 110."  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>id</em>., at 996a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNother1.16" href="#FRother1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The court's exchange with Juror 20 (who sat on the jury) is typical:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Q. Do you remember reading any particular articles about this case or Mr. Lay or Mr. Skilling?</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A. Not until just recently this week, but nothing--</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Q. And there have been a lot of articles this week.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A. Yeah.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Q. Do you recall any particular articles you've read in the last week or so?</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A. Not word for word, no.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Q. Did you read all the articles in the Sunday "Chronicle"?</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A. Some of them.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Q. Which ones do you remember reading?</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"A. The one about the trial, I think, and how the trial was going to work."  <em>Id</em>., at 873a-874a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNother1.17" href="#FRother1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority's criticism of Skilling's counsel for failing to ask questions of many of the prospective jurors, cf. <em>ante</em>, at 23-24, is thus misplaced.  Given the District Court's express warning early in the <em>voir dire</em> that it would not allow counsel "to ask individual questions if [they] abuse[d]" that right, App. 879a, counsel can hardly be blamed for declining to test the court's boundaries at every turn.  Moreover, the court's perfunctory exchanges with prospective jurors often gave counsel no clear avenue for further permissible inquiry.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNother1.18" href="#FRother1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although the District Court underestimated the extent of the community hostility, it was certainly aware of the ubiquity of the pretrial publicity, acknowledging that "all of us have been exposed to substantial media attention about this case."  <em>Id</em>., at 841a.  The court even made an offhand remark about one of the prior Enron prosecutions, "the Nigerian barge case," apparently expecting that the prospective jurors would understand the reference.  <em>Id</em>., at 840a.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNother1.19" href="#FRother1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Taking a more defendant-favorable line than most prospective jurors, Juror 17 stated that he "thought the guy [on the radio] was pretty narrow minded," that "everyone should be considered innocent totally until they get a chance to come [to] court," and that the Government might have been overzealous in some of its Enron-related prosecutions.  <em>Id</em>., at 863a-864a.  He added, however, that he "believe[d] there was probably some accounting fraud [at Enron]."  <em>Id</em>., at 864a.  The District Court denied the Government's request to remove Juror 17 for cause, but he did not ultimately sit on the jury.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNother1.20" href="#FRother1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority attempts to downplay the significance of Juror 29 by noting that she did not end up on the jury because Skilling used a peremptory challenge to remove her.  See <em>ante</em>, at 30, n. 32.  The majority makes a similar point with respect to other venire members who were not ultimately seated.  See <em>ante</em>, at 24, n. 24.  The comments of these venire members, however, are relevant in assessing the impartiality of the seated jurors, who were similarly "part of a community deeply hostile to the accused" and who may have been "unwittingly influenced by it."  <em>Murphy</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 421 U.&nbsp;S. 794, 803 (1975); see also <em>Irvin</em> v. <em>Dowd</em>, 366 U.&nbsp;S. 717, 728 (1961).  Moreover, the fact that the District Court failed to remove persons as dubiously qualified as Juror 29 goes directly to the adequacy of its <em>voir dire</em>.  If Juror 29 made it through to the end of the selection process, it is difficult to have confidence in the impartiality of the jurors who sat, especially given how little is known about many of them.  Cf. 6 LaFave 23.2(f), at 288 ("The responses of those not seated casts light on the credibility of the seated jurors who were familiar with the same publicity").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNother1.21" href="#FRother1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority also notes that about two-thirds of the seated jurors and alternates (11 of 16) had no personal Enron connection.  <em>Ante</em>, at 24, and n.&nbsp;25.  This means, of course, that five of the seated jurors and alternates did have connections to friends or colleagues who had lost jobs or money as a result of Enron's collapse--a fact that does not strike me as particularly reassuring.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNother1.22" href="#FRother1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As one of Skilling's jury experts observed, there is a "tendency in voir dire of jury pool members in high-profile cases to minimize their exposure to media, their knowledge of prejudicial information, and any biases they may have."  App. 763a; see also <em>id</em>., at 637a ("Those who perceive themselves or wish to be perceived as good citizens are reluctant to admit they cannot be fair").  For this reason, the fact that "none of the seated jurors and alternates checked the 'yes' box" on the written questionnaire when "asked whether they 'ha[d] an opinion about [Skilling],'&nbsp;" <em>ante</em>, at 26, is of minimal significance, particularly given that the Causey plea and the impending trial received significant media coverage <em>after</em> the questionnaires were submitted.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNother1.23" href="#FRother1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Many other seated jurors and alternates expressed similarly troubling sentiments.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Supp. App. 57sa-60sa (Juror 20) (obtained Enron-related news from the Chronicle<em> </em>and "local news stations"; blamed Enron's collapse on "[n]ot enough corporate controls or effective audit procedures to prevent mismanagement of corporate assets"; and was "angry that so many people lost their jobs and their retirement savings"); <em>id</em>., at 72sa-75sa (Juror 38) (followed Enron-related news from various sources, including the Chronicle; was "angry about what happened"; and "fe[lt] bad for those that worked hard and invested in the corp[oration] only to have it all taken away"); <em>id</em>., at 117sa-118sa (Juror 64) (had several friends who worked at Enron and lost money; heard about the Enron cases on the news; described the collapse as "sad" because "people lost jobs [and] money--lots of money"; and believed the Government "did the right thing" in its investigation); <em>id</em>., at 177sa-181sa (Juror 87) (received Enron-related news from the Chronicle, Channel 13 news, the O'Reilly Factor, Internet news sources, and friends, family, and co-workers; attributed Enron's collapse to "[p]oor management [and] bad judgment--greed"; lamented "[t]he sad state of the long-term loyal employees who are left with nothing in their retirement accounts"; and "admire[d] [the] bravery" of Enron whistle-blower Sherron Watkins "for bringing the situation to the attention of the public, which stopped things from getting worse"); <em>id</em>., at 191sa-195sa (Juror 90) (heard Enron-related news from his wife, co-workers, and television; wrote that "[i]t's not right for someone ... to take" away the money that the "small average worker saves ... for retirement all his life"; and described the Government's Enron investigation as "a good thing"); <em>id</em>., at 221sa-225sa (Juror 113) (obtained information about Enron from a "co-worker [who] was in the jury pool for Mrs. Fastow's trial"; worked for an employer who lost money as a result of Enron's collapse; found it "sad" that the collapse had affected "such a huge number of people"; and thought "someone had to be doing something illegal"); <em>id</em>., at 236sa-237sa (Juror 116) (knew a colleague who lost money in Enron's collapse; obtained Enron-related news from the "Houston Chronicle, Time Magazine, local TV news [and] radio, friends, family, [and] co-workers, [and] internet news sources"; and noted that what stood out was "[t]he employees and retirees that lost their savings").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNother1.24" href="#FRother1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Several other jurors fell into this category.  Juror 67 wrote on his questionnaire that he had heard about Enron from the Chronicle<em> </em>and "Internet news sources."  <em>Id</em>., at 133sa.  He was questioned for 90 seconds, during which time he indicated that he had read an article on the Internet the preceding night "about the jury selection taking place today, stuff like that."  App. 944a.  Juror 99 wrote that she had not heard or read about the Enron cases and did not "know anything about" Enron.  Supp. App. 210sa.  The District Court questioned her for barely one minute.  She stated that she had "[n]ot really" learned more about the case, but added that she had heard "this and that" from her parents.  App. 995a-996a.  The court did not press further.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNother1.25" href="#FRother1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority suggests that the fact that Skilling "challenged only one of the seated jurors for cause" indicates that he did not believe the other jurors were biased.  <em>Ante</em>, at 31.  Our decisions, however, distinguish claims involving "the partiality of an individual juror" from antecedent claims directed at "the partiality of the trial jury as a whole."  <em>Patton</em> v. <em>Yount</em>, 467 U.&nbsp;S. 1025, 1036 (1984); see also <em>Frazier</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 335 U.&nbsp;S. 497, 514 (1948) ("[T]he two sorts of challenge[s] are distinct and are therefore to be dealt with separately").  If the jury selection process does not, as here, give a defendant a fair opportunity to identify biased jurors, the defendant can hardly be faulted for failing to make for-cause challenges.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>

<div class="caseInformation section">

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> SKILLING <em>v</em>. UNITED STATES (2010)</h2>
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Docket No:</b> No. 08-1394</p>
        
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Argued:</b> March 01, 2010</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Decided:</b> June 24, 2010</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Court:</b> United States Supreme Court</p>
    </div>

<div class="cardLinks list section"><div class="fl-card">
    <div class="fl-card__header">

</main><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';
  var c = _satellite.cookie.get(cookieName);
  // we use a cookie to ensure multiple beacons are not sent for the same ad
  if( typeof c == 'undefined' ) {
    _satellite.setFlCookie(cookieName, cookieValue, 1);

    var data = {};
    var clickUrlElement = document.getElementById(_satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub'));
    if (clickUrlElement !== null) {
      data.clickUrl = clickUrlElement.innerText;
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') ) {
      data.adDecisionContext = _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id') ) {
      data.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub') ) {
      data.subId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id') ) {
      data.flUserId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_tag_track') == 'false' ) {
      data.internalFlag = 'true'; // mimick as an internal request
    }

    let url = new URL(window.location.href);
    let domain = 'attorneys.findlaw'+(_satellite.getVar('cms_env') == 'prod' ? '' : '-stage')+'.com';
    let attorneysUrl = new URL('https://'+domain+'/flstats/v1/stats');
    let searchParams = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);

    if (searchParams.has('ads-server-env')) {
      attorneysUrl.searchParams.set('ads-server-env', url.searchParams.get('ads-server-env'));
    }
    navigator.sendBeacon(attorneysUrl.href, JSON.stringify(data));
  }
}

    expireDays = dt.toGMTString();
  }
  document.cookie = cookieName+"="+cookieValue+"; expires="+expireDays+'; path='+path+'; domain='+_satellite.getVar('host_root');
};

    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');

          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }

    })

      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search

      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });

          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
